0685054667|5.0|I adore Anne Frank: The Diary of a Young Girl and have read it multiple times over the years. Anne's diary has come to mean different things to me as I've grown older - when I first read it as a ten-year-old girl, I was fascinated by the hidden life she led with her family and there was a sense of dread and excitement about her precarious circumstances, the danger of being discovered. When I read Anne's diary again as a teenager, I came to empathize with Anne's frustrations with her mother and the other adults around her whom Anne felt did not understand her and treated her as a child. I felt Anne's desire to be 'heard' and not just treated as an inconvenience or child was so much aligned with what I was going through during adolescence.<br /><br />When I re-read the diary as an adult, I came to appreciate all the other things - the tragedy of having lost such an amazing and talented young voice in the most horrible of circumstances; the beauty of Anne's writing which is all the more amazing given how young she was when she wrote this diary; and the themes of alienation, fear, and hope. Now, as the mother of a young daughter, I read this diary again and gain another fresh perspective - of the complex relationship between daughter and mother. Anne had a strained relationship with her mother Edith, and clearly gravitated toward her beloved Pim (father). This was obvious even before Anne and her family were compelled to move into hiding in the Annex. As a mother, reading Anne's thoughts about her mother (often unflattering) was a disconcerting experience, though things do improve somewhat later on.<br /><br />Anne Frank's Diary of a Young Girl is a classic not only of Holocaust literature, but also a classic Young Adult read with its myriad themes that will appeal to both adolescents and adult readers.

1451627289|3.0|I have two problems with this novel - A minor issue is that the characters are pretty much stereotypes, and therefore not very engaging, but a larger issue is that King falls back on the \\"Occam's Razor\\" argument to justify a PC perspective on the assassination. This is a pet peeve of mine. Occam's Razor doesn't apply to human motivation. People regularly hatch convoluted plans in order to promote an agenda. The evidence is in the complexity and stubborn anti-nature orientation of our society. Human behavior doesn't meld with or reflect activities in the natural world. A childlike, simple-minded, or psychologically unsophisticated person might not understand this, but Stephen King is none of these. He even creates a metaphor for this in his  repeated emphasis of the air and water pollution in the 50's and 60's. Then he fails to follow through with his almost-theme.  He could have extended this into human psychology, pointing out that it's not only the past that's \\"obdurate\\" - his vocabulary lesson for the populace. Will we now see the word used more often? - and that people stubbornly believe what is convenient and non-threatening to them. Instead, he rests his plot on the PC perspective on the events of 11/22/63, then rationalizes this in a half-apologetic, half-defensive Afterward.   Being a person who leaves the \\"Occam's Razor\\" argument where it belongs - in the self-regulating natural world - I wonder if King's novel would have been publishable if Jake Epping had followed the rabbit hole to Lyndon Johnson and associates. Perhaps not - especially going into an election year. But leaving aside \\"conspiracy theories\\" (that discredited and outlandish idea that human beings conspire to gain wealth and power,) a more generous explanation for King's literary sellout to the 5th grade version of U.S. history might simply be that a world in which people conspire to engage in coup d'etat would have required a much more complex plot, not to mention more character developmenton the part of his hero. It was undoubtedly easier to have Jake Epping remain safely asleep in the arms of the American propaganda cult.   All in all, this novel seemed to me like a tremendous waste of a great plot idea. However, there were some points where I laughed aloud, especially when King had the old guy make short work of the time-travel chestnut about the danger of killing your own grandfather, by saying, \\"Why the f____ would I want to do that?\\"   To be fair, King's job is to write stories, not confront issues that are preventing this country from facing nearly-insurmountable problems. I'll try not to hold it against him, though I am disappointed that, in the less overtly befouled air of the present day, one can't get a whiff of moral courage in a novel, let alone in real life.

B004EPYZXU|2.0|I originally saw this film displayed at the checkout counter at Rite Aid, so my expectation of the film was low. I bought it realizing that sometimes these unknown films are hidden treasures. Apollo 18 was not one of those films. The one thing I have to commend the filmmakers for was creating a realistic depiction of the 70's space program. The grainy simulated stock footage certainly gave the impression the footage was actually from the 70s. I should mention the production design and the simulated lunar surface have never been more realistically created as they are in this film. The filmmakers obviously studied meticulously the Apollo flights, which are now approaching forty years old.  Obviously, the filmmaker's eye for realism was done in order to make this a realistic horror film in the tradition of the Blair witch project, and several paranormal activity movies.  However, Apollo 18 falls short in the area of drama. There are only a few shocks in the film, while the rest of the story malingers on. Apparently, the astronauts along with a Soviet counterpart craft are sent to the moon to expose a contaminated life form. The astronaut's nemesis turns out to be spider-like creatures that hatch out of lunar rocks. To say this is ludicrous is an understatement. For the rest of the film basic science is thrown out the window and replaced with cheap thrills. The spider creatures themselves are generic looking CGI effects. The rest of the film resembles Alien, where the space spiders chase the hapless astronauts through the ship. None of the astronauts are allowed home.  How could the filmmakers in this day and age even contemplate any living organism on the moon? Maybe if the film was set on Mars it would at least be a bit more believable. This plot scenario would have worked better in the 50s, but today it is just silly. Then again there are those who still believe we did not land on the moon. Maybe this is the type of film they would enjoy.
B003K16P3M|5.0|All over the Seven Kingdoms, the Gracelings come into their Grace. A Grace can be anything from climbing trees to baking to swimming and more. Identified by their mismatched eyes, Gracelings are revered by Kings for their usefulness and rejected by the common people for their abnormalities. Graceling takes us into the world of Katsa, graced with killing. From a young age, Katsa was subjected to the cruel whims of her uncle King Randa. After accidentally killing her cousin for his groping hands, Katsa began training to become the King's enforcer.   Katsa, becoming tired of following unjust orders from her uncle, establishes the Council. The Council is an extensive network of people who use the information they gather to stop the power-hungry kings from abusing their people. The Council uses information to prevent villages from being massacred, people from being executed, and rescues those kidnapped by opposing kingdoms. The latter of these begins a series of events that will change Katsa forever.   After rescuing a Lienid prince from the dungeon of a neighboring kingdom and disabling all of the guards between the dungeon and her escape, Katsa encounters a mysterious Lienid. Instead of raising the alarm, he allows Katsa to flee, not before she knocked him out of course.   Upon returning to her uncle's kingdom, she discovers the stranger is already there. Not only is he a Lienid prince, but is searching for his grandfather, the very one she rescued.  Graceling was a book that my brother originally suggested to me, and I was going to eventually read it, but seeing reviews on it propelled me to pick it up. I was extremely engrossed by the concept of Gracelings and their different expressions, as well as how they can change over time. Katsa is a lovably guff heroine, who could probably kill with both arms tied behind her back and legs bound at the ankles, but struggles with her sense of self and her place in the world. As the book progresses, Katsa learns to stand up forherself and let down the walls around her heart that she so carefully constructed. Watching the relationship between Katsa and the other characters evolve from a simple acquaintance to true friendship is thrilling.   I would highly recommend this book to readers 14 and up as the author suggests. I found that being older while reading it allowed me to grasp the concepts better and more fully understand the struggles Katsa has as she discovers who she is and what she wants out of life. There is a bit of material that I wouldn't recommend for younger readers, but nothing too graphic, and the underlying tension would be difficult for young readers to pick up on. While the complexity and sheer GROOVE of the instrumentation may overshadow the deceptively simple lyrics, there is ALWAYS a great story there, one that is imbued with the verisimilitude of gender, race, and class struggles of post-modern American culture.
